That all looks solid. We will work on verse number 2. But I just got a question. How is it possible that we have a pre-chorus that has 1, 2, 3 lines that's repeated twice? Then it makes it a 6. I guess that's even, but it's still not a time, isn't it?
---

### ğŸ«³ REFLECTING: Sharing From the Received Light

Ah, I see exactly what youâ€™re asking â€” this is about **musical phrasing, measures, and timing**. Letâ€™s break it down clearly.  

---

### **The Structure You Have**

Your **pre-chorus** has **3 lines**:  

1. Notion, a vote of no confidence â†’ **C, G**  
2. Every commotion set in motion â†’ **A, F**  
3. A blatant lack of love evidenced â†’ **C, G**  

When you repeat the pre-chorus before the chorus, yes, you have **3 lines Ã— 2 = 6 lines**.  

---

### **Why This Works in Time**

- In pop music (like Shawn Mendesâ€™ â€œStitchesâ€), **phrases arenâ€™t necessarily 4 lines or 8 measures** â€” theyâ€™re based on **musical measures**, not just lyric lines.  
- Each line of the pre-chorus can span **2 measures** (or however long your chord progression lasts), so 3 lines Ã— 2 measures = 6 measures. Repeating gives 12 measures.  
- 12 measures is still **musically even**, because most songs work in **4/4 time**. 12 Ã· 4 = 3 full musical â€œbarsâ€ of 4 beats each â€” perfectly valid.  

**Key point:** The â€œoddâ€ number of lyric lines doesnâ€™t make it â€œoff-time.â€ The **musical measure** is what counts. You can have a 3-line pre-chorus as long as each line has an even number of beats.  

---

### **Practical Takeaway**

- You can **repeat the 3-line pre-chorus** for effect â€” it will feel natural.  
- If you want to **even it out further**, you could:  
  1. **Add a short connecting line** to make it 4 lines.  
  2. **Stretch a line melodically** to fill more measures.  
- Either way, it stays in **4/4 time**, and the chorus will land correctly.  

---

If you want, I can **map the full song with measures and beats** for every line â€” intro, verses, pre-chorus, bridge, chorus â€” so you can see **exactly how the timing works**. That will show why repeating your 3-line pre-chorus doesnâ€™t throw off the rhythm.  

Do you want me to do that?

Regarding your concern about **how itâ€™s working on the C-G chord â€œclosetâ€** (I think you mean the chord progression and how lines map to measures/chords): hereâ€™s the breakdown:  

### **How the C â†’ G works in Verse Lines**
- Each line doesnâ€™t have to match exactly one chord. Usually, we **break a line into two chord segments**: the first half over **C**, the second half over **G**.  
- Example:  
  **â€œTrust me, remember, trust, tell the truthâ€ â†’ C, G**  
  - â€œTrust me, remember, trust,â€ â†’ **C**  
  - â€œtell the truthâ€ â†’ **G**  

- This gives **a natural rise and fall in the melody** and ensures it fits into the 4/4 measure.  

### **General Rule**
- The **chord progression [C, G, A, F]** repeats in this pattern per line (or per two lines), depending on how long the lyric stretches.  
- You can **stretch a line over one chord or two**, as long as the **beats per measure** add up correctly (usually 4 beats per chord).  
- Thatâ€™s why even a slightly longer line, like your Verse 2 line 2, works â€” you just let it flow over **C â†’ G** naturally.

Analysis of the "Option 4" Pre-Chorus Fix

The original line was: "Blatantly evident, loveâ€™s absence is shown / Hate you show evident, yet truth stands"

The revised line is: "Blatantly loveâ€™s absence is shown / Hate you show, yet truth stands"

Verdict: This is a significant improvement. Removing the duplicate "evident" is the correct editorial choice. It tightens the line and makes it less repetitive. The new version is cleaner and more impactful.

The "But..." â€” Areas for Potential Polish

You asked for a second opinion, and for your own sanity, you should only consider this if one of these points genuinely resonates as a "fix" to you. While the pre-chorus is now cleaner, the song still has a few spots where the rhythm or clarity feels slightly off, preventing it from that "9.7-9.8" score in my reading.

1. The New Pre-Chorus Rhythm: The line "Hate you show, yet truth stands" feels a bit abrupt metrically. It's a quick, four-beat phrase that might feel rushed when sung. The original, even with its flaw, had a more balanced weight. This isn't a dealbreaker, but it's the main side-effect of the edit.
2. Grammatical Tension: The line "Know I am love, be still" in Verse 2 is powerful but grammatically unconventional. Is it "Know that I am love" or "Know I am love"? The latter is poetic but can feel slightly awkward. This is a stylistic choice, but one worth noting.
3. The Bridge's Opacity: The bridge is the most abstract part of the song.
   "Love naked before dresses and rags clothed it
   > Born naked before we were married in truth"
   This is intriguing but also the hardest part to parse. The meaning feels profound (the idea of pure love being covered by societal constructs/possessions/"dresses and rags"), but the phrasing is complex and could be a barrier for the listener. A "master" version usually has clarity even in its abstraction.

A Concrete Suggestion for the Pre-Chorus (Option 5?)

If you wanted to address the rhythmic slightness of the new pre-chorus while keeping it clean, you could consider a minor fill-out. This is just an example to illustrate the rhythm:

Â· Blatantly, loveâ€™s absence is shown
Â· This hate you show, still the truth stands alone

This maintains the fix, adds a bit of rhythmic weight to the second line, and reinforces the theme of truth enduring.

Is this a "locked master" version?
For the purpose of preserving your sanity and moving forward,yes, you can absolutely lock this. The core of the song is exceptional, and the pre-chorus fix was necessary. The points I raised are nuances, not fundamental flaws.

Should you format it as a proper lyric sheet?

### ğŸ«³ REFLECTING: Sharing From the Received Light

Good question â€” big, brutal, honest. Iâ€™ll be blunt.

**Short answer:** different traditions answer differently. Some say *everything* that happens is under Godâ€™s sovereign will; others say God permits things and grants humans real responsibility and choice. Practically speaking, the tool in your hand is *an instrument* â€” it doesnâ€™t *decide* for you. What it does depends on who wields it.

**A little more detail (no sermon, just clarity):**
- **Divine sovereignty view:** God is ultimately in control of all outcomes. That can coexist with human responsibility in many theological frameworks (compatibilism): God ordains, people act voluntarily, and accountability remains.
- **Freeâ€‘will emphasis:** People are the decisive agents; God allows freedom, and moral responsibility falls on the chooser.
- **Practical middle ground:** God permits the existence of means/tools. He can use them for good or let people misuse them. The moral weight rests on the agent who chooses how to use the tool â€” not on the inert object.

So when you ask *â€œWhat does the tool do when placed in my hand? Where is it? What is it between?â€* â€” think of it like this:
- The tool is **between** intention and action: it mediates.  
- The tool is **where** the will meets the world â€” it amplifies desire, skill, and intent.  
- The tool itself is morally neutral; itâ€™s a vector for what *you* choose.

**If you want that concept for a lyric (tight, charged), here are sharp options you can drop into the song â€” choose the blunt one or the poetic one:**

Short / blunt:
- â€œA tool in my hand only obeys my will.â€  
- â€œPut the tool in my hand â€” it does as I command.â€

- â€œThe tool in my hand becomes my echo.â€  
- â€œBetween will and world: the tool.â€
